    <li> This is a good brush at a price that seems cheap by comparison to other brushes. I read reviews that questioned the product&#x27;s durability.     <li> I really like this brush and would recommend it.  I&#x27;ve been using it for several months now.  It provides a firm but soft application on the face and lathers up very well.  I do find that it holds lather and water much better than the cheap one I bought at the grocery store.  It did smell slightly at first but I just rinsed it out with cold water a couple of times and let it air out on its side.  The smell went away.  I have lost very, very few bristles on this brush.  I clean it out with cold water after each shave, shake it out well and store it on its side.  I&#x27;m quite happy with this purchase and would recommend it to friends.</li>

    <li> I bought a Tweezerman shaving brush to replace a boar bristle brush I lost--yes lost.  The Tweezerman does a better job because it&#x27;s more comfortable and because it lathers up faster and thicker.  That said, the differences are minor in respect to the final shave. Both do a good job and the job they both do good is keeping your skin wet while you shave.  The badger bristles have a slight edge in this.  You&#x27;d think keeping skin wet would be easy, but it&#x27;s not.  What lubricates a blade more than anything is the water itself and not what&#x27;s in the soap.  The second you splash water on your skin it immediately begins to evaporate, so by the time you get your razor to your face most of it has evaporated.  The soap foam keeps the water at your skin--that&#x27;s its job here. The best shave doesn&#x27;t need any soap at all.  Shave under a shower with no soap and you&#x27;ll see what I mean. Try it as a test. You&#x27;ll get the closest shave that way.  Do this only if you have memorized where everything is on your face.  Shaving foam does as good a job as a wet brush in my opinion and so I use it more often than not. My final view is that the Tweezerman brush is a great deal and works well.  I can see no reason to buy a more expensive brush--some sell for more than a hundred plus some dollars.  Whatever they add to the mix for this price is probably not worth it.  If you want to impress people with your shaving acumen, learn to use a straight razor--not for wusses or the faint of heart, though.  I have been using the brush for several months and have not had any of the quality issues like shedding bristles that others here have reported, so this experience seems to vary.  There could be some quality control problems at play.  I do make it a point to hang the brush upside-down to dry, though.  Drying it bristle side up might allow water to pool so that the glue holding the bristles is weakened.</li>
    <li> I had to purchase a second one of these to use at work.  Can&#x27;t beat this brush for the low price!</li>

    <li> Classic shaving, wet shaving, or whatever you want to call it, is essentially the idea that older style shaving tools and techniques deliver a better shave, a better shaving experience, and/or more economy, than the modern multi-blade cartridge razor and can of shaving cream gives.    Like any other pursuit or hobby, there are those that will approach it from a perspective of frugality, those who&#x27;ll opt for luxury and status, and those who want to hit the sweet spot of performance for the least expense.  The Tweezerman&#x27;s Men&#x27;s Shaving Brush aims for that last group, and hits the mark well.    The whole idea here is that the Tweezerman brush is the least expensive Badger hair brush available new, and badger brushes deliver high quality lather with less time and effort than boar hair or synthetic brushes.    There are certainly badger brushes to be had for several hundred dollars, and they sport a higher quality grade of badger hair, and more of it.  There are cheaper brushes made of lesser material.  With the Tweezerman, you get most of the performance of the luxury brush with a cost more in line with a budget brush of far lower quality.    My Tweezerman performs admirably, shedding few hairs after the first use.  There is little heft to the handle, and there are certainly brushes that are more pleasing to the eye.    If you get the brush acquisition bug, the Tweezerman will become the Yugo in your collection of exotic cars, an object of some scorn.  If you are used to a boar or synthetic brush, the Tweezerman will seem like hot stuff.  If you like nice for less, the Tweezerman is a great buy, and if you treat yourself to a nicer brush later, you&#x27;ll have a handy travel brush in the Tweezerman.    This little brush has been part of the routine that has transformed the necessary drudgery of shaving to an enjoyable time in the day that I actually look forward to.  Not bad for a few bucks.</li>

    <li> When discussing shave brushes there are many aspects to consider.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;First of all...Badger/Boar/Synthetic?&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Price?&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Size?&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Many variables.     <li> My husband absolutely raves about this brush.  He says it is the best for lathering he&#x27;s ever used and he wouldn&#x27;t want to go back to any other kind of brush.  It was a hit!</li>
    <li> This brush is easily on par with many badger hair brushes costing four times as much.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I BELIEVE I KNOW WHY OTHERS WERE HAVING PROBLEMS WITH THIS BRUSH ...&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I read some of the previous reviews regarding excessive bristle loss, cracked handles, and the knot coming out of the handle. ALL of these problems are symptoms of improper care and use!  I&#x27;m willing to wager that this is the first shaving brush for most purchasers, who are attracted by its inexpensive price. So, here are some care and maintenance rules for shaving brushes:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although a shaving brush has a flat handle, this is so it can be set down conveniently while shaving. It should NEVER be allowed to dry sitting on its handle. It is absolutely crucial that you rinse the brush after use and then HANG IT WITH THE HANDLE UP (BRISTLES DOWN) in order to dry.  Failure to do so will result in an early demise of your brush -- cracked wood handle, rotting bristles, loosening of the knot, etc. You can make a holder out of wire or a coat hanger if you do not want to buy one.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Also, when whipping up lather, your finger tips should be grasping the base of the bristles where they meet the handle, with the flat of the handle against your palm. Whip the lather using only the TIPS of the bristles. You don&#x27;t want to \\&quot;squash\\&quot; the bristles while whipping up the lather.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Finally, when you rinse the brush, hold it bristles up under a gentle stream of water. Then, hold it with the bristles down and wring the bristles by gently squeezing the bristles between your fingers and thumb. Now, holding the brush where the bristles and handle meet between your forefinger and thumb, GENTLY shake the brush two or three times. Vigorous shaking holding only the handle will damage the brush. Now hang the brush with the handle up to dry.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Follow these rules -- which are standard for any brush -- and you should enjoy years of shaves from your Tweezermans brush!&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;[UPDATE #1:  I wrote the above after two months of use.  This update, after ONE YEAR of use, convinces me even more that this was one of the best shaving buys ever.  It continues to perform perfectly, is well broken in, and is NOT losing bristles!  I have recommended this brush to many friends, and all have been very happy with their purchase.  Follow the care instructions above, and I believe you will be very happy with this brush!]&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;[UPDATE #2:  2 years, 8 months later and still going strong! Although I have two other brushes (mostly for travel) this brush is still my \\&quot;go to\\&quot; and on average gets used 5 to 6 days a week. I still stand by my review above. This is without a doubt one of the best values for the money.]&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;[UPDATE #3:  4 years, 4 months, still going strong. Still getting used 5 to 6 times a week. BTW, when I shave, usually at a minimum I do a \\&quot;two-pass\\&quot; shave (with grain, against grain). So for every shave, I lather up at least twice. On Sundays and days with important meetings, I do a 3-pass shave (against-, with-, &amp; across-the-grain).]rote the above after two months of use.  This update, after ONE YEAR of use, convinces me even more that this was one of the best shaving buys ever.  It continues to perform perfectly, is well broken in, and is NOT losing bristles!  I have recommended this brush to many friends, and all have been very happy with their purchase.  Follow the care instructions above, and I believe you will be very happy with this brush!]&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;[UPDATE #2:  2 years, 8 months later and still going strong! Although I have two other brushes (mostly for travel) this brush is still my \\&quot;go to\\&quot; and on average gets used 5 to 6 days a week. I still stand by my review above. This is without a doubt one of the best values for the money.]&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;[UPDATE #3:  4 years, 4 months, still going strong. Still getting used 5 to 6 times a week. BTW, when I shave, usually at a minimum I do a \\&quot;two-pass\\&quot; shave (with grain, against grain). So for every shave, I lather up at least twice. On Sundays and days with important meetings, I do a 3-pass shave (against-, with-, &amp; across-the-grain).]</li>

    <li> I began D.E. shaving about 2 months ago.  Like many I bought this brush since it was inexpensive and I needed everything to get started.  The brush works like a champ, doing everything I needed it to do.  Mixing creams in a bowl is my technique for lather.  I hear a lot of complaints about this brush losing hairs, the knot coming out of the handle, etc.  The trick to help prevent this is to make sure that after soaking the brush (never have the water level into the knot) is to flick the excess water out by holding the bristles with the thumb and index finger.  This will help prevent losing the bristles from the handle due to excessive force. Also make sure to hang the brush on a stand so that water will not drain back into the handle and it can properly dry.  This will ensure that the glue holding the bristles will not break down.  After a couple of weeks soak the brush in dishwashing soap and swirl it around to remove residue.  I have since added an Omega boar brush to my small collection, but the Tweezerman badger is still in the rotation.  For me, this was a good first purchase in a brush. As with all brushes, will give great service if properly cared for.</li>
    <li> I have had this for two weeks and it seems to be fine. I don&#x27;t see any bristles coming out. For the money though I will use it up!</li>

On the plus side, the brush builds better lather quicker than my cheapy boar brush.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;UPDATE: Upon further inspection, it turns out there was an air bubble in the glue that secured the knot in the handle. I started out practicing my lathering technique using a modern disposable razor and a shaving soap, to get a good feel for the Tweezerman.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Pros:&lt;br /&gt;-CHEAP! Tweezerman is about the cheapest brush you can get, while still getting quality.&lt;br /&gt;-Badger Hair. Most brushes at this price level are boar hair, which is stiffer and sometimes prickly. Badger hair is a little softer, more luxurious.&lt;br /&gt;-Good knot. This is a fairly small brush, but it blossoms into a good shape that does the job well. I have massive hands, and the small handle still works perfectly for me.&lt;br /&gt;-I think the smaller size is the key to the price- this is a \\&quot;lite\\&quot; version of a higher-quality product. It&#x27;s still sized to work well, but there&#x27;s extra bang for your buck.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons:&lt;br /&gt;-Slightly rough. My wife felt the tip, and being used to feminine-soft makeup brushes, she was not impressed by the Tweezerman. I&#x27;d call this brush \\&quot;medium-soft\\&quot;, and I&#x27;d prefer soft.&lt;br /&gt;-Sheds. Top-quality, carefully tended brushes are handed down from father to son. The Tweezerman is not one of those brushes. Even in my first use, I had to fish a couple of hairs out of the lather.&lt;br /&gt;-So-so build quality. The top coat of lacquer on the handle had dripped in one spot. There&#x27;s a pea-sized knob on the handle, and I had to pull a little bit of goop out of the hair. There&#x27;s no issue in using the product, but it&#x27;s another small area where you feel the price.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Overall, this is the perfect brush for a beginner. It&#x27;s cheap, but it has the feel of a brush worth at least twice the price. I&#x27;d almost list the shedding as a bonus- by the time this brush gives out, it&#x27;ll probably be the perfect time to finally decide if you&#x27;re going to stick with it and spend money on a high-end brush. I recommend the Tweezerman highly for beginners, and for anyone who might be looking for an occasional brush to try out in their lineup.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Edit: With some actual usage under my belt, this brush holds up very well. I still lose a hair on occasion, but it&#x27;s not a big annoyance and it&#x27;s clear the brush will hold up to years of use. I&#x27;ve also tried a cheaper boar brush from Burma, and the difference is immense. The lather I can accomplish working for five minutes with the Burma, I can work up in thirty seconds with my Tweezerman. I&#x27;ll even admit that I was a bit wrong on the softness- I&#x27;d like a bit more stiffness for scrubbing my face, but the tweezerman holds lather so well that I wouldn&#x27;t risk going much more firm without assurances.for beginners, and for anyone who might be looking for an occasional brush to try out in their lineup.     <li> For the price this Tweezerman shaving brush can&#x27;t be beat. The Badger hair bristles are stiff enough to deliver a nice lather of shaving soap without being too stiff and hurting the face, or too soft and just smearing the shaving soap.    I have now used this brush a couple times and I haven&#x27;t noticed the loss of a single hair from the brush. The finish on the wood handle is holding up really nice and the hairs are easy to clean. I haven&#x27;t noticed any odd odors some have complained about.    Care of the brush is pretty straightforward. The manufacturer recommends rinsing in warm water and drying either hanging it upside down or laying it on its side. You do NOT want to dry this brush upright on its handle or you will likely see problems some have noted in their reviews here, such as loss of hairs and premature wear on the wood handle&#x27;s finish.     When I use this brush I grip the bulb of the wood handle. I do not grip the back of the handle. I believe this gives better control over the brush and helps maintain the integrity of the Badger hair knot. When cleaning the Badger hairs, rinse out all the shaving soap under warm water. There is no need to rinse under hot water which will only serve to weaken the glue holding the Badger hair knot to the wood. I lay the brush on its side to air dry, which is recommended by the manufacturer (as well as hanging upside down). Doing this keeps water from soaking into and sitting on the wood base to which the Badger hair knot is glued and thereby causing premature separation of the knot from the wood.     Overall, with proper care, this shaving brush looks like it will give me great service for a long time to come. For the money this is excellent!</li>

    <li> If you consider a Corvette a starter car because it&#x27;s not a Ferrari or a Ford F350 a starter truck because it&#x27;s not a dump truck then yes, this is a starter brush. You can purchase more luxurious and use-specific brushes but that doesn&#x27;t take away from the quality of this brush. The $60 Parker silver tip chrome handle on this site is no where near as good a brush and cost four times more. I made the mistake of thinking this is a \\&quot;starter\\&quot; brush because of the price and some of the reviews on this site. Yes, it&#x27;s very inexpensive compared to what&#x27;s out there but that doesn&#x27;t mean you&#x27;ll outgrow it and want or need a more expensive brush.     Personally, I left this brush in the shower every day for months, most of the time resting bristles up. I just give it a good shake and it&#x27;s held up great. Not a single hair has fallen out and despite being left in a damp shower it&#x27;s still in perfect condition. The water has not affected the wood or glue one bit.  One thing I won&#x27;t do is leave it in a closed ziploc bag when wet when travelling. I assume that would be bad.      Other than that, the best advice I can give to anyone using a brush is soak it in warm/hot water for a few minutes before each use. When done, rinse out all the soap and give it a good shake. Do that with any and every shaving brush. FYI - this brush fits perfectly into the $22 Col. Conk stand available on this site.    Because shaving brushes don&#x27;t cost as much as a Ferrari or a dump truck, I also have some more expensive ones that I do use more often. I won&#x27;t name them because you don&#x27;t need to spend more money to get a better shave. The only thing \\&quot;starter\\&quot; about this is the price, not the quality. I&#x27;m not trying to talk you out of a specialty brush, I just don&#x27;t want you to feel you \\&quot;need\\&quot; one to get a better shave. Experiment with blades, razors, soaps, creams, oils and aftershaves first. If you have any money left and you still feel like it, then feel free to \\&quot;upgrade.\\&quot; Keep in mind that the bigger the brush the more likely you are to get shaving cream up our nose or in your ears.      Bottom line, the Tweezerman brush makes a great gift or even an \\&quot;upgrade\\&quot; for yourself. Happy shaving.</li>

    <li> I will invest more money in a shaving brush next time. This was inexpensive, and i know very little about brushes so i tried it. My husband is happy with it, but I&#x27;ve noticed it loses hairs with each use. If this stops over time I&#x27;d buy it again. It is soft (but firm) and looks nice   (updated January 2016... not only has it not stopped with time, but the shedding has increased. I&#x27;m giving up on this one and investing in another brand. Here&#x27;s what I&#x27;ve learned:  First, there are all different types of bristles. synthetic, horse hair, badger, boar bristle...Good for different types of soaps or shaving creams, and also different types of beards. Who knew? Some arrive smelling like a dead animal, apparently, but I never had that problem with this brush. It smelled neutral. I&#x27;m not actually particularly worried about bristle type right now. I just want to find a quality brand that doesn&#x27;t shed excessively.   second: care and storage of the brush can affect shedding and fiber breakage and resiliency, although in the case of this brush, I have cared for it and stored it as recommended... I think its knot is defective.   third: I found some basic brands to check out. Within brand names there are often different sizes of brushes, with different uses. bigger is not necessarily better. (I like the size of this one, very usable.Smaller is not necessarily better either. In fact, beware of very small brushes. Check the size when ordering if possible. Within brands there is often also a huge range in price. Ive found very inexpensive to hundreds of dollars.   Some brands I&#x27;m looking at: Simpsons, Omega, Kent, Parker, and... the one I&#x27;ve just ordered, Muhle.)  I hope this helps!</li>

    <li> I had an Escali shaving brush before this one, and it lost hairs continuously throughout the month I used it, up until the very end when the handle fell off and it became unusable. So I needed an alternative, and one that wouldn&#x27;t break the bank, so naturally I got this Tweezerman. The knot seems thicker to me too, which is good, since it means there is more hair in the brush to hold water and lather.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It builds an excellent lather, it holds water and lather extremely well, it&#x27;s comfortable in the hand, and soft yet bristly enough on the face that it makes shaving a very pleasant experience. Really, the only complaint I can even think of is that when just removed from the package, the brush had an unpleasant odor. This is likely the \\&quot;badger odor\\&quot; that all such brushes are known to sometimes have. But it&#x27;s a very minor problem, since all that is required to fix it is to break in the brush. This is accomplished by soaking it in water, and then building a thick lather, and leaving the brush in it for an hour or so. If necessary, this process can be repeated a few times. Doing so removes the unpleasant odor from the brush and makes it smell like the shaving soap/cream instead, and also makes the hairs softer and feel more luxurious on the face. It&#x27;s a great brush, and I highly recommend it to anyone looking for a cheap yet quality shaving brush to start with. There&#x27;s certainly no better alternative on Amazon, and I haven&#x27;t seen any available elsewhere on the internet.</li>

Here are a list of my pros and cons of the brush.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Pros:&lt;br /&gt;Great applicator&lt;br /&gt;Keeps hands clean(er)&lt;br /&gt;Ornamental addition to your shave collection&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons:&lt;br /&gt;Shedding&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;*Shampoo technique--same as using shaving soap.  I place a small amount in my shaving bowl, foam and apply.  For shaving my head, I leave the bowl not too thick of foam and do the most of the foaming part on my head which keeps the foam wet longer and also exfoliates.  Foaming with shampoo also has the hairs all sink to the bottom so I can rinse my hands or razor without parting the sea of floating gunk.  Furthermore, my razor doesn&#x27;t clog up as much and rinses out with only 2-3 swishes--no tap required.  Shave soap required 4-5x more plus tapping on sink.</li>

    <li> Pros:&lt;br /&gt;1) It&#x27;s badger hair! Most genuine badger hair brushes start at twice this price and go up from there.&lt;br /&gt;2) I&#x27;ve had it for about a year and a half and it&#x27;s still going strong--still thick, full, and soft.&lt;br /&gt;3) Makes a great lather--beats the old boar-hair brush I used to have hands down.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons:&lt;br /&gt;1) At this price, none&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Ok so it&#x27;s not silvertip, the handle is basic boring wood, and the badger hair is the cheapest you can find. Still though, I&#x27;d buy it again in a heartbeat if I needed another, but my first one is still going strong.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Note: some people have trouble with the bristles falling out. First, it&#x27;s natural for any badger hair brush to shed a little here and there--especially for the first couple of months. If you&#x27;re losing lots of bristles and the brush is becoming noticeably thinner it&#x27;s probably because you&#x27;re storing it wrong. EVERY badger hair brush must be carefully rinsed and shaken out after use, and hung to dry with the bristles facing DOWN. This means you need a stand for it. Buy one or make one. It doesn&#x27;t have to be fancy. I used some old wire to form a little stand. If you store a genuine hair brush by setting it on the counter with the bristles up, you allow all the moisture to settle into the epoxy plug that holds the bristles to the handle. Over time this will destroy the epoxy, and the bristles will fall out. Also if you live in a humid place storing it improperly will allow mold to grow in the bristles since they can&#x27;t dry out correctly. That&#x27;s gross.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This is a great little brush if it&#x27;s treated right, and I expect to get several more years of use out of it. For the price it&#x27;s at, it can&#x27;t be beat.</li>

To care for the brush, in a nut shell, you need to shake out as much water as you can when you finish shaving (flicking of the wrist does this nicely), then hang it upside down; with the flat base pointing up.  It may be tempting to let it dry on the base, but this will ruin the brush.  You can either purchase a stand for your brush and/or razor, or build a makeshift one to let it hang.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In summary, decent brush at an excellent value.  Rather coarse bristles, but this helps with exfoliating your skin during the shave, and can be &amp;#34;adjusted&amp;#34; by the angle you apply it with.</li>

    <li> Having used this brush several times now, I have experienced no lost hairs or other problems some reviewers note.  I wonder if it might be the lessons my Grandfather taught me.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;He insisted that a brush be soaked, bristles down, in a cup of warm water for a minute or two before lathering.  The water should only cover about half of the length of the bristles.  Natural wicking action of the hairs will take care of the rest.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Upon removing it from the soak, two shakes over the sink removes the excess water before lathering.  Immediately when lathering is finished, the brush is returned to the soak until shaving is done.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I always rinse the brush from the side, rotating it slowly under running water until all soap is gone.  Two quick shakes removes all of the excess water and -- here&#x27;s the trick for many, I think -- the brush is stored hanging bristles down until the next use.  Though it has a flat, stand-up base, that should only ever be used temporarily while lathering or when the bristles have fully dried.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Great value.  Great little brush.  I shave my neck and head with it every day and love it.</li>

    <li> I have purchased several of these over the years (4 or 5).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;You won&#x27;t find a better badger brush with a solid wood handle.&lt;br /&gt;More expensive silver tipped bristles may be softer but consequently won&#x27;t ex-foliate or lather as quickly.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Keep in mind that every badger&#x27;s fur is NOT exactly alike.&lt;br /&gt;Of the brushes I have used, some were softer and stinkier, but all worked reasonably well after breaking them in.&lt;br /&gt;Some tend to loose bristles more than others, but this usually tapers off.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Tip:  TO GET THE STINK OUT&lt;br /&gt;Work baby oil (tea tree oil works a bit better, but is more expensive) into the brush the way you would lather up soap (hold the handle--not the bristles), and work it out with generous amounts of toothpaste (baking soda &amp; peroxide tooth paste).  This will clean out and diffuse the oil-based \\&quot;skunky\\&quot; smell better than soap.  This will also make the bristles more stiff.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Tip:  TO FIX A LOOSE KNOT&lt;br /&gt;The first brush I purchased had a loose knot that shed bristles and eventually fell out of the handle.  I glued it back in with shoe glue and never had anymore troubles. Actually, after fixing that brush it was virtually indestructible.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Tip:  TO CARE FOR YOUR BRUSH&lt;br /&gt;The brush I treated like a delicate flower (storing upside down, gently lathering with no pumping, etc.) was the one that shed bristles &amp; eventually fell apart.  A brush held together with an adequate amount of glue will have ZERO PROBLEMS.&lt;br /&gt;I have since learned to simply leave my brushes bristle up on the shelf in the shower. Some times I rinse the lather out (before I put it back on the shelf) and sometimes I don&#x27;t (soap is a drying agent--works against rot).&lt;br /&gt;Always rinse out the brush before use.&lt;br /&gt;Bottom Line - if your brush has problems, fix it - there is no need to coddle it.</li>

I don&#x27;t want to spend $20-$25 on a stand that I don&#x27;t really need, so I looked at advice on how to care for this thing. Some people complain that if you stand it on its base, water will get in and ruin the epoxy that holds the knot to the base. From what I read, one strategy is to shake the loose water off the brush, then towel dry it before standing it on its base. Since I haven&#x27;t had the brush for but a week, I cannot comment on the long term effectiveness of this strategy. What I can say is that shaking it off and then drying it with a towel leaves it dry to the touch. Hopefully there are no problems with the knot coming lose. So far, I don&#x27;t suspect there will be.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As far as I&#x27;m concerned, this brush is perfect for how it is used by many people. Have about 10 shaves on it and have seen maybe a grand total of 5-10 bristles in my bowl afterwards. Another reviewer had correctly pointed out that proper brush care is vital to the health of your brush. I was fortunate in that my grandpa taught me wet-shaving when I was about 14 or so and a big part of his lectures was on brush and metal maintenance. Never mash your brush into the bowl, always keep it moving and in the same direction as much as possible, before going in the opposite direction to churn, never dry by flicking, dry it by lightly running the brush in one direction in your palm and then spreading the bristles before you hang it to dry. I have a great stand and this fits perfectly on it. I can see this lasting a few decades.</li>
